5) Heir release (MVP â†’ V2)

6) Privacy model (kept simple)

Project Title: Firefly Grove

Purpose and Vision:
Firefly Grove is a private memory journal that helps people preserve stories, photos, and voice notes about the people and moments that shaped their lives. Each person or relationship becomes a â€œbranch.â€ Each memory inside that branch glows like a small firefly. The goal is to build a tool that feels emotionally warm and personalâ€”not like social mediaâ€”and allows controlled sharing and legacy release after someone passes away.

Development Environment:
All development will take place inside Claude Code. Claude will write, refactor, and document the code. The finished application should run as a Node.js web app that can be deployed easily to Vercel or another neutral host such as Render or Fly.io. No Google Cloud services or Google APIs will be used.

Technology Stack:

Backend: Node.js (v18+) with Express or a lightweight HTTP framework.

Database: Postgres (Supabase, Neon, or Vercel Postgres).

File Storage: Backblaze B2, Wasabi, or Bunny.net (any S3-compatible provider).

Front End: Plain HTML, CSS, and vanilla JavaScript for MVP; responsive layout and calm animation.

Payment System: Stripe for $9.99/year subscriptions.

Email Delivery: Resend or SendGrid for transactional messages such as invites, billing notices, and legacy releases.

Backups: Weekly export of Postgres data and file metadata to Backblaze B2 with Object Lock (write-once protection).

Version Control: GitHub repository connected to Vercel for deployment automation.

Core Features:

1. Branches
Each branch represents a person, relationship, or theme. Users can create, name, and describe a branch.

2. Memories
Each branch holds multiple memories. A memory can contain text, an optional photo, and an optional audio recording. Each memory includes: visibility (private, shared, or legacy), creation date, and author.

3. Visibility and Permissions

Private: only the owner sees it.

Shared: visible to invited members of that branch.

Legacy: hidden until a release condition is met (for heirs).
Each branch has one owner who controls permissions. Invited members can add memories, but their entries remain pending until approved.

4. Legacy Release
Owners can designate heirs by email and define release conditions such as â€œafter death,â€ â€œafter a chosen date,â€ or â€œmanual release.â€ When triggered, the system exports the relevant branch to a static archive (ZIP or PDF) and emails heirs a secure download link.

5. Billing and Access Control
Stripe manages subscriptions. Active users can create and edit branches. Expired or unpaid users enter a locked state where they can view but not add or edit. A grace period of 30 days applies.

6. Forever Kits (Export)
Any branch can be exported to a downloadable â€œForever Kitâ€ containing all its memories and media. The same archive is stored in immutable backup storage.

7. Demo Mode
Set by environment variable DEMO_MODE=true. In demo mode, the app seeds fake users (e.g., Alice and Bob) and example branches (like â€œZachary Petersonâ€). External servicesâ€”Stripe, email, and file storageâ€”are bypassed. The admin can switch between fake users and simulate billing or legacy releases.

Data Model Overview:

Users: id, email, name, status (active, locked, legacy), created_at.

Branches: id, owner_id, title, description, status, created_at.

Entries: id, branch_id, author_id, text, visibility, legacy_flag, media_url, created_at.

BranchMembers: branch_id, user_id, role (owner or guest), approved.

Heirs: branch_id, heir_email, release_condition, notified.
Media files stored as /userID/branchID/entryID/filename.

User Interface:

Home Screen: dark background with softly glowing fireflies. Each firefly represents a branch; brightness or size reflects recent activity.

Branch Screen: zooms into one branch showing smaller fireflies for individual memories.

Memory View: opens a single entry with text, photo, and audio playback.

Prompts: rotating text such as â€œDescribe a small thing that made you laugh today.â€

Settings Panel: handles exports, heirs, and subscription renewal.
Visual tone should be quiet, intimate, and minimal.

Automations and Jobs:

Monthly Integrity Check: verifies backup hashes.

Legacy Monitor: checks release conditions and triggers heir notifications.

Scalability and Limits:

Design and Tone Guidelines:

Developer Brief: Memory Deletion and Relationship Control System

Purpose

This brief defines UX, permissions, data model changes, API behaviors, storage handling, audits, and tests. It assumes Node.js backend, Postgres, S3-compatible storage, Resend/SendGrid, Stripe, and Vercel.

Core Principles

Clarity over cleverness: users always know what will happen to a memory.

Recoverable by default: nothing vanishes instantly unless explicitly â€œpermanent.â€

Least privilege: contributors control their own posts; owners control the branch; admins are safety net only.

Emotional tone: â€œWithdraw,â€ â€œArchive,â€ and â€œHideâ€ beat â€œDeleteâ€ for everyday actions.

User Roles and Authorities
â€¢ Contributor: may create memories in branches theyâ€™re invited to; can withdraw their own memories; can report content.
â€¢ Branch Owner: full control within the branch; can archive the branch; can permanently delete any memory in the branch.
â€¢ Admin: global override for abuse, legal, and emergency cleanup; minimal use.
â€¢ Heirs (legacy release): read-only access post-release; no deletion.

Actions and UX States

â€¢ Immediate Undo: after creating or uploading, show a 60-second â€œUndoâ€ banner on the memory card.
â€¢ Disable double-submit: disable upload button on first click; show spinner and progress; re-enable when server confirms.
â€¢ De-dupe guard: back end rejects duplicate upload within 30 seconds for same user, branch, filename, and file hash; return 200 with existing record idempotently.

Level 2: Relationship Change (respectful control)
â€¢ Withdraw Memory (Contributor): hides contributorâ€™s memory from everyone else. Card remains for contributor with status â€œWithdrawn (only you can see this).â€
â€¢ Hide from View (Owner): hides a memory from branch members without deleting.
â€¢ Archive Branch (Owner): branch becomes private and non-discoverable; members lose access; owner retains full access; exports still allowed.

Level 3: Abuse/Defamation (safety net)
â€¢ Report Memory: any member can report; reasons: harassment, private info, spam, other.
â€¢ Admin Review: admin can Hide, Permanently Remove, or Dismiss with note.
â€¢ Tombstone: optional placeholder â€œRemoved by admin on <date>â€ to keep audit continuity, no media.

â€¢ Owner or Admin only. Two-step confirmation with typed phrase (â€œpermanently removeâ€).
â€¢ Removes DB record and associated objects from storage after retention window (see Retention).
â€¢ Not available for Legacy-released content unless admin override with reason and export snapshot created first.

â€¢ memories: add columns

â€¢ reports: new table

Permissions Logic (server enforced)
â€¢ Read: owner sees all non-deleted; members see shared and non-hidden; contributors see their own withdrawn items.
â€¢ Write:

â€¢ Legacy mode: before release, legacy memories behave like private; after release, heirs get read-only; no deletes by heirs.

API Endpoints (concise specs)
â€¢ POST /memories/:id/undo

Window: 60 seconds after create; reverts create; idempotent.
â€¢ POST /memories/:id/withdraw

â€¢ POST /memories/:id/hide

â€¢ POST /memories/:id/restore

â€¢ POST /branches/:id/unarchive

â€¢ POST /reports

â€¢ GET /trash

â€¢ On create: store original and normalized versions; record content_hash.

â€¢ Permanent Remove: â€œThis will permanently delete this memory and its files after the retention window. This cannot be undone.â€
â€¢ Archive Branch: â€œYour branch becomes private. Members lose access until you unarchive.â€

Rate Limits and Safety Rails
â€¢ POST /memories create: max 5 per minute per user per branch; return 200 on replay with same content_hash within 30 seconds.
â€¢ Report spam control: captcha or friction after N reports per hour per user.
â€¢ Destructive actions require re-auth if session older than 1 hour (step-up auth).

â€¢ Undo banner removes memory within 60 seconds.

â€¢ Owner can hide and restore any memory; members lose and regain access appropriately.
â€¢ Owner can permanently delete; files purged after retention; restore not possible post-purge.

â€¢ Report flow: member reports; admin action hides/deletes/dismisses; notifications sent.
â€¢ Audit records present for every action.
â€¢ Legacy branch: heirs read-only; cannot delete; owner actions still allowed pre-release.
â€¢ Storage: normalized and original variants removed on purge; backups remain intact by policy.

Migration Plan (zero downtime)

Ownerâ€™s Policy (plain language, for Terms/Help)

Title: Grove Implementation with Stripe and Tree Capacity (Unlimited Branches per Tree)

Key Definitions

Tree: A sub-container within a Grove, typically representing a family line or household. Unlimited Branches live here.

Family Grove: $9.99/year, capacity 10 Trees, unlimited Branches per Tree.
Ancestry Grove: $19.99/year, capacity 25 Trees, unlimited Branches per Tree.
Institutional Grove: $99/year, capacity 100 Trees, unlimited Branches per Tree.

Data Model (additive)
groves: id; owner_user_id; name; plan_type (family, ancestry, institutional, trial); tree_limit (int); stripe_customer_id; stripe_subscription_id; status (active, past_due, canceled); created_at; updated_at.

On successful checkout:

Capacity Enforcement (Trees)

Enforcement on Subscription State

ğŸ§© Conceptual Principle

âš™ï¸ How It Works in Practice

ChatGPT said:
Perfectly timed question â€” this is the economics and fairness rule of the Grove system, and it has big implications for how it grows sustainably.

Hereâ€™s the breakdown of your scenario, both philosophically and technically:

ğŸŒ³ The Principle
Each Groveâ€™s tree capacity is meant to represent how many connections (trees) you maintain â€” not global ownership of the person.
So yes, you each â€œspendâ€ one tree slot when you choose to include that person in your Grove.

âš™ï¸ The Logic Behind It
Letâ€™s use your example:

Mom owns a Family Grove (10 trees) and gifts a tree to her son.

You own a Family Grove (10 trees) and gift a tree to your grandson â€” the same person.

Both of you are using one of your tree slots because:

Each Grove is a container of relationships, not a global directory.

That person (your grandson) exists in both contexts with the same global person_id, but he occupies a slot in both Groves since both of you are maintaining separate sets of branches and memories tied to him.
